do_single_rollout

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. For example, \[f(2) = \underbrace{(1 + 2 + 1)}_{\text{2nd row}} - \underbrace{(1 + 1 + 1)}_{\text{0th and 1st rows}} = 1. \]What is the minimum value of $f(n)$ for $n \ge 2015$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: By symmetry of the triangle, $f(n) = 0$ for all those $n$. \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Thus the sum of the elements of the $k$th row, for $k \le n$, is $2^k$, so the total sum of all the elements of the previous rows is \[\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} 2^k = \frac{2^n-1}{2-1} = 2^n-1\] by the formula for the sum of a geometric series. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. For example, \[f(2) = \underbrace{(1 + 2 + 1)}_{\text{2nd row}} - \underbrace{(1 + 1 + 1)}_{\text{0th and 1st rows}} = 1. \]What is the minimum value of $f(n)$ for $n \ge 2015$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We can check all the values for small $n$ and we find that the minimum values occur at $n = 2, 4, 5, 203, 206$. It will thus be unnecessarily large to check possible values for $\boxed{2015}$, and instead we only need to extend our search for all the previous minimum values, that is $n=203, 206$. For the latter, we find $f(206) = -4015707$, which is negative, and the minimum value is \boxed{ 4015707 }. The sum of digits in $\boxed{4015707}$ is $23 + 18 + 7 = 48$. \boxed{48}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Thus the sum of the elements of the $k$th row, for $k \le n$, is $2^k$, so the total sum of all the elements of the previous rows is \[\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} 2^k = \frac{2^n-1}{2-1} = 2^n-1\] by the formula for the sum of a geometric series. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. For example, \[f(2) = \underbrace{(1 + 2 + 1)}_{\text{2nd row}} - \underbrace{(1 + 1 + 1)}_{\text{0th and 1st rows}} = 1. \]What is the minimum value of $f(n)$ for $n \ge 2015$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: $f(n)$ is a positive integer because for any $n \ge 0$, its value is 1 or 0 plus the positive sum of at most $n$ elements from a row of Pascal's triangle, which must be a number of the form $1 + 2 + 3 + \ldots + k \le \frac{2k(k+1)}{2}$. Thus $f(n) \ge 0$ for all $n$. Therefore $f(n)$ cannot be less than 1, and \textbf{the minimum value of } $\boxed{1}$. \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Thus the sum of the elements of the $k$th row, for $k \le n$, is $2^k$, so the total sum of all the elements of the previous rows is \[\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} 2^k = \frac{2^n-1}{2-1} = 2^n-1\] by the formula for the sum of a geometric series. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Many numbers have the period $1$ (named after the Conjectures of Nicholas Roerich), which counts the number of ones and ones without zeros in the decimal expansion. In particular, the decimal expansion of $1$ looks like 1.1 1.11 1.111 1.1111 1.11111..., and one can check that for every integer $n$, the 25th term is the same as the $(27 \cdot 2^n)$th term. \boxed{1}

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: This is a known sequence of integers; for any $n$ we have $f(n) = \frac{1}{n+1}$. When this sequence reached $2015$, we have \boxed{2013 11/3}. Thus $f(2015) = 1/2015 < 1/2013 = 2/2268 = 1/1134.$

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Looking at $f(5), f(4), f(3), f(2), f(1)$, we see: \begin{align*} f(5) &= 1 + 3 - 3 = 1 \\ f(4) &= 3 + 4 - 4 = 3 \\ f(3) &= 0 + 2 - 2 = 0 \\ f(2) &= 1 + 3 - 3 = 1 \\ f(1) &= 0 - 0 = 0 \end{align*} The smallest possible value we can get for $f(n)$ is when $n = 2$. \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We only need to evaluate $f(3037), f(3038), \ldots, f(2015)$, namely the first $1031$ rows. Notice that if $f(n) = f(m)$ for $n \ge m$, the $n$th row contributes $f(n)$ to the sum for $n \ge 0$. Furthermore, we see that $f(1) = 1$. Hence, the smallest possible negative sum can be obtained by summing up the first $1031$ rows and writing the sum as a polynomial: \boxed{-\tfrac{1}{1012} \cdot 1 \cdot 1031 = -\tfrac{1}{1012}}. \boxed{-\dfrac{1}{1012}}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. For example, \[f(2) = \underbrace{(1 + 2 + 1)}_{\text{2nd row}} - \underbrace{(1 + 1 + 1)}_{\text{0th and 1st rows}} = 1. \]What is the minimum value of $f(n)$ for $n \ge 2015$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We can write the formula for the entire first $n$ rows as $p_n = \binom{n+1}{i+1} + \binom{n+1}{i} + 1$ for $0 \le i \le n$. This means that $f(n) = p_n - p_{n - 1}$. The minimum value of $f(n)$ occurs when $n$ is the largest value for which $p_n \le 2p_{n-1}$, i.e. when $\binom{n+1}{i+1} + \binom{n+1}{i} \le 2 \binom{n}{i}$. Since $\binom{n+1}{i+1} \binom{n}{i} = \frac{n+1}{i+1}$, this inequality holds when $n \le 1996$. \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Thus the sum of the elements of the $k$th row, for $k \le n$, is $2^k$, so the total sum of all the elements of the previous rows is \[\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} 2^k = \frac{2^n-1}{2-1} = 2^n-1\] by the formula for the sum of a geometric series. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

Problem: For each integer $n$, let $f(n)$ be the sum of the elements of the $n$th row (i.e. the row with $n+1$ elements) of Pascal's triangle minus the sum of all the elements from previous rows. For example, \[f(2) = \underbrace{(1 + 2 + 1)}_{\text{2nd row}} - \underbrace{(1 + 1 + 1)}_{\text{0th and 1st rows}} = 1. \]What is the minimum value of $f(n)$ for $n \ge 2015$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We can write out Pascal's triangle before simplifying: \[1 + 1 + 1 = 1, 2 + 1 + 1 = 2, 2 + 2 + 1 = 3, 3 + 2 + 1 = 4, \dots \] At each step, we can only increase the middle sum. We can thus make the ans 2015 and other larger values of $n$ continue to have a middle sum of 2048. \boxed{966}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

For example, \[f(2) = \underbrace{(1 + 2 + 1)}_{\text{2nd row}} - \underbrace{(1 + 1 + 1)}_{\text{0th and 1st rows}} = 1. \]What is the minimum value of $f(n)$ for $n \ge 2015$? Response: $f(n)$ is the sum of the central $n+1$ items in any row of Pascal's triangle. The central item in the $n+1$th row is $1 \cdot 1 = 1$ and so $f(n+1) = 0$. Therefore the sequence $f(n)$ is $1, 0, 0 \dots$ \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Suppose $m \ge 1$. The sum of the elements of the $m$th row is simply \[\sum_{i=0}^{m} \binom{m}{i} = \sum_{i=0}^m \binom{m}{i}1^i = (1+1)^m=2^m\] by the binomial theorem. Thus the sum of the elements of the $k$th row, for $k \le n$, is $2^k$, so the total sum of all the elements of the previous rows is \[\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} 2^k = \frac{2^n-1}{2-1} = 2^n-1\] by the formula for the sum of a geometric series. Therefore, $f(n)=2^n-(2^n-1)=1$ for all $n \ge 2015$. (That is, it is a constant!) Thus, the minimum value is clearly $\boxed{1}$.

For an element to have order 10, the LCM of the orders of \(a\) and \(b\) must be 10. 2. **Possible Orders**: - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \), possible orders of elements are divisors of 30: 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, 10, 15, 30. - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{120} \), possible orders of elements are divisors of 120: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 8, 10, 12, 15, 20, 24, 30, 40, 60, 120. 3. **Pairs of Orders with LCM 10**: We need pairs \((m, n)\) such that \( \text{LCM}(m, n) = 10 \). The valid pairs are: - \((1, 10)\), \((2, 5)\), \((2, 10)\), \((5, 2)\), \((5, 10)\), \((10, 1)\), \((10, 2)\), \((10, 5)\), \((10, 10)\). 4. **Counting Elements**: - For each pair \((m, n)\), count the number of elements in \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \) with order \(m\) and in \( \mathbb{Z}_{120} \) with order \(n\). - Use the Euler's totient function \(\phi\) to count elements of specific orders. 5. **Number of Elements**: - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \): - \(\phi(1) = 1\), \(\phi(2) = 1\), \(\phi(5) = 4\), \(\phi(10) = 4\). - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{120} \): - \(\phi(1) = 1\), \(\phi(2) = 1\), \(\phi(5) = 4\), \(\phi(10) = 4\). 6. **Total Elements of Order 10**: - Calculate the product of counts for each pair \((m, n)\) and sum them up: - \((1, 10)\): \(1 \times 4 = 4\) - \((2, 5)\): \(1 \times 4 = 4\) - \((2, 10)\): \(1 \times 4 = 4\) - \((5, 2)\): \(4 \times 1 = 4\) - \((5, 10)\): \(4 \times 4 = 16\) - \((10, 1)\): \(4 \times 1 = 4\) - \((10, 2)\): \(4 \times 1 = 4\) - \((10, 5)\): \(4 \times 4 = 16\) - \((10, 10)\): \(4 \times 4 = 16\) - Total: \(4 + 4 + 4 + 4 + 16 + 4 + 4 + 16 + 16 = 72\). 7. **Number of Cyclic Subgroups**: - Each cyclic subgroup of order 10 has \(\phi(10) = 4\) generators. - Number of distinct cyclic subgroups: \(72 / 4 = 18\). Thus, the number of distinct cyclic subgroups of order 10 in \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \oplus \mathbb{Z}_{120} \) is \(\boxed{18}\).

For an element to have order 10, the LCM of the orders of \(a\) and \(b\) must be 10. 2. **Possible Orders**: - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \), possible orders of elements are divisors of 30: 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, 10, 15, 30. - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{120} \), possible orders of elements are divisors of 120: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 8, 10, 12, 15, 20, 24, 30, 40, 60, 120. 3. **Pairs of Orders with LCM 10**: We need pairs \((m, n)\) such that \( \text{LCM}(m, n) = 10 \). The valid pairs are: - \((1, 10)\), \((2, 5)\), \((2, 10)\), \((5, 2)\), \((5, 10)\), \((10, 1)\), \((10, 2)\), \((10, 5)\), \((10, 10)\). 4. **Counting Elements**: - For each pair \((m, n)\), count the number of elements in \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \) with order \(m\) and in \( \mathbb{Z}_{120} \) with order \(n\). - Use the Euler's totient function \(\phi\) to count elements of specific orders. 5. **Number of Elements**: - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \): - \(\phi(1) = 1\), \(\phi(2) = 1\), \(\phi(5) = 4\), \(\phi(10) = 4\). - In \( \mathbb{Z}_{120} \): - \(\phi(1) = 1\), \(\phi(2) = 1\), \(\phi(5) = 4\), \(\phi(10) = 4\). 6. **Total Elements of Order 10**: - Calculate the product of counts for each pair \((m, n)\) and sum them up: - \((1, 10)\): \(1 \times 4 = 4\) - \((2, 5)\): \(1 \times 4 = 4\) - \((2, 10)\): \(1 \times 4 = 4\) - \((5, 2)\): \(4 \times 1 = 4\) - \((5, 10)\): \(4 \times 4 = 16\) - \((10, 1)\): \(4 \times 1 = 4\) - \((10, 2)\): \(4 \times 1 = 4\) - \((10, 5)\): \(4 \times 4 = 16\) - \((10, 10)\): \(4 \times 4 = 16\) - Total: \(4 + 4 + 4 + 4 + 16 + 4 + 4 + 16 + 16 = 72\). 7. **Number of Cyclic Subgroups**: - Each cyclic subgroup of order 10 has \(\phi(10) = 4\) generators. - Number of distinct cyclic subgroups: \(72 / 4 = 18\). Thus, the number of distinct cyclic subgroups of order 10 in \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \oplus \mathbb{Z}_{120} \) is \(\boxed{18}\).

Problem: Determine the number of distinct cyclic subgroups of order 10 in the group \( \mathbb{Z}_{30} \oplus \mathbb{Z}_{120} \). Response: A Generator (or cyclic subgroup) is an element \( a \) that can generate all the members of a subgroup \( Z \) by using the operation of \( Z \): \( Zn = la^n = a\(a^n\) \). A cyclic subgroup is a group that requires only \(a\) to generate all of its members. \boxed{4} )

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

Problem: Given a positive real number $x$, a sequence $\{a_n(x)\}$ is defined as follows: \(a_1(x)=x\) and \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\) recursively for all \(n\geq 2\). Determine the largest value of $x$ for which \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We need to find the limit given the fact that \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\), and \(x\) is a positive real number. Taking each term of the \(n\)-th term and squaring them and simplifying, we get \[\begin{align} a_n(x)&=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\\ a_n(x)^2 &= x^{a_n*{a_{n-1}(x)}}\\ a_n(x)^2 &= x^{a_{n-2}(x)}\\ a_n(x)^4 &= x^{a_{n-2}(x)}\\ a_n(x)^{2^n} &= x\\ a_n(x) &= x^{1/2^n}\\ \lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x) &= 1\\ \boxed{1}\end{align}\] \boxed{1}

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

do_single_rollout

Problem: Given a positive real number $x$, a sequence $\{a_n(x)\}$ is defined as follows: \(a_1(x)=x\) and \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\) recursively for all \(n\geq 2\). Determine the largest value of $x$ for which \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Summing the series \(1+x+x^2+\dots+x^N\), we may divide by \(1-x\), giving 1/\(1-x\). This series converges for all \(|x|<1\). The series 1/\(1-x\) converges when \(|x|<1\) and diverges when \(x>1\). \boxed{1}

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

Problem: Given a positive real number $x$, a sequence $\{a_n(x)\}$ is defined as follows: \(a_1(x)=x\) and \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\) recursively for all \(n\geq 2\). Determine the largest value of $x$ for which \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: To have a limit as we take $n \to \infty$, each successive $a_{n-1}$ must approach a value different from $x$, which means the limit should be either $0$ or $1$. \boxed{1}

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

Problem: Given a positive real number $x$, a sequence $\{a_n(x)\}$ is defined as follows: \(a_1(x)=x\) and \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\) recursively for all \(n\geq 2\). Determine the largest value of $x$ for which \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Clearly, \(\lim_{n\to\infty}x^{x^{...}}=\lim_{n\to\infty}x^{x^{x^{...}}}=\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists, no matter what $x$ is. \boxed{Z}

Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

Problem: Given a positive real number $x$, a sequence $\{a_n(x)\}$ is defined as follows: \(a_1(x)=x\) and \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\) recursively for all \(n\geq 2\). Determine the largest value of $x$ for which \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: For all \(x\geq 1\), \(a_n(x)\geq x\), hence \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)=1\) (except possibly at \(x=1\)). Thus the largest value of \(x\) is \(1\). \boxed{1}

Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

Problem: Given a positive real number $x$, a sequence $\{a_n(x)\}$ is defined as follows: \(a_1(x)=x\) and \(a_n(x)=x^{a_{n-1}(x)}\) recursively for all \(n\geq 2\). Determine the largest value of $x$ for which \(\lim_{n\to\infty}a_n(x)\) exists. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We have $a_n(x)=x^{x^{...}}$. For $x \lt 1$, the term gets an exceedingly large exponent and is near zero, so for the limit to exist we know $x\gt 1$. \boxed{1}

This sequence forms an infinite power tower \( x^{x^{x^{\cdot^{\cdot^{\cdot}}}}} \). 1. **Fixed Point Equation**: If the limit \( L \) exists, it must satisfy \( L = x^L \). Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we get \( \ln L = L \ln x \), which can be rewritten as \( \frac{\ln L}{L} = \ln x \). Therefore, \( x = L^{1/L} \). 2. **Maximizing \( x \)**: To find the maximum \( x \), we need to maximize the function \( h(L) = L^{1/L} \). Taking the natural logarithm of \( h(L) \), we get \( \ln h(L) = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). Let \( y = \frac{\ln L}{L} \). The derivative of \( y \) with respect to \( L \) is: \[ \frac{dy}{dL} = \frac{1 - \ln L}{L^2} \] Setting the derivative to zero gives \( 1 - \ln L = 0 \), which implies \( L = e \). Substituting \( L = e \) back into \( h(L) \), we get: \[ h(e) = e^{1/e} \] 3. **Convergence Analysis**: For \( x = e^{1/e} \), the fixed point equation \( L = x^L \) becomes \( L = e^{L/e} \). Solving this, we find \( L = e \). The derivative of the function \( f(L) = x^L \) at \( L = e \) is \( f'(e) = e^{1/e} \cdot \ln(e^{1/e}) = 1 \). Although the derivative is 1, numerical simulations suggest the sequence converges to \( e \) very slowly. 4. **Divergence for \( x > e^{1/e} \)**: For \( x > e^{1/e} \), the sequence \( \{a_n(x)\} \) diverges to infinity as shown by numerical examples (e.g., \( x = 1.5 \)). Thus, the largest value of \( x \) for which the limit exists is \(\boxed{e^{1/e}}\).

Problem: In Pascal's Triangle, each entry is the sum of the two entries above it. In which row of Pascal's Triangle do three consecutive entries occur that are in the ratio $3: 4: 5$? (The top row of Pascal's Triangle has only a single $1$ and is the $0$th row.) Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The ratio holds in the $4$th, the $8$th, and the $15$th rows. \boxed{15th}

Reference Answer: Let the three entries be $\binom{n}{r},$ $\binom{n}{r+1},$ and $\binom{n}{r+2},$ respectively. Then we have \[\frac{\binom{n}{r}}{\binom{n}{r+1}} = \frac{3}{4} \quad \text{and} \quad \frac{\binom{n}{r+1}}{\binom{n}{r+2}} = \frac{4}{5}.\]We simplify the left-hand side of the first equation: \[\frac{\binom{n}{r}}{\binom{n}{r+1}} = \frac{\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}}{\frac{n!}{(r+1)!)(n-r-1)!}} = \frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!} \cdot \frac{(r+1)!(n-r-1)!}{n!} = \frac{r+1}{n-r}.\]Therefore, $\frac{r+1}{n-r} = \frac{3}{4}.$ Similarly, the second equation becomes $\frac{r+2}{n-r-1} = \frac{4}{5}.$ Cross-multiplying in both equations, we have \[4r+4 = 3n-3r \quad \text{and} \quad 5r+10 = 4n-4r-4.\]Solving for $r$ in the first equation gives $r = \frac{3n-4}{7},$ and then we have \[9\left(\frac{3n-4}{7}\right) + 14 = 4n,\]and solving for $n$ gives $n = \boxed{62}.$

Problem: In Pascal's Triangle, each entry is the sum of the two entries above it. In which row of Pascal's Triangle do three consecutive entries occur that are in the ratio $3: 4: 5$? (The top row of Pascal's Triangle has only a single $1$ and is the $0$th row.) Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The $3, 4,$ and $5$ are in the $2$nd row.\boxed{2}

Reference Answer: Let the three entries be $\binom{n}{r},$ $\binom{n}{r+1},$ and $\binom{n}{r+2},$ respectively. Then we have \[\frac{\binom{n}{r}}{\binom{n}{r+1}} = \frac{3}{4} \quad \text{and} \quad \frac{\binom{n}{r+1}}{\binom{n}{r+2}} = \frac{4}{5}.\]We simplify the left-hand side of the first equation: \[\frac{\binom{n}{r}}{\binom{n}{r+1}} = \frac{\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}}{\frac{n!}{(r+1)!)(n-r-1)!}} = \frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!} \cdot \frac{(r+1)!(n-r-1)!}{n!} = \frac{r+1}{n-r}.\]Therefore, $\frac{r+1}{n-r} = \frac{3}{4}.$ Similarly, the second equation becomes $\frac{r+2}{n-r-1} = \frac{4}{5}.$ Cross-multiplying in both equations, we have \[4r+4 = 3n-3r \quad \text{and} \quad 5r+10 = 4n-4r-4.\]Solving for $r$ in the first equation gives $r = \frac{3n-4}{7},$ and then we have \[9\left(\frac{3n-4}{7}\right) + 14 = 4n,\]and solving for $n$ gives $n = \boxed{62}.$
